RATING: 6.5
Pure abstract with excellent components and very good scalability (1-4).

Another need to win by having the highest total of your lowest scores like in Tigris & Euphrates, but of course the actual gameplay is completely different.  Seems kind of like a more strategic Blokus- you can block other players and set up good areas for yourself.  This game really works because it has some depth, it's very easy to learn and jump in and play, and you can even play it by yourself!  I'm not normally a fan of pure abstracts, but this is a solid title.

RATING: 7
2008-09-02
One of the best abstract games.

2016-03-24
The secret of Ingenious lies in its scoring system. You cannot concentrate on one thing only, and this makes the game very interesting, since even if one player scored a ridiculous amount of points in category, s/he might still not win the game. Moreover, the accumulation of big scoring chances create a natural dramatic arc to the game with a lot of tension and big climaxes. 

The game scales well to varying player numbers and playing time is just right. Of course, with more players the game tends to become more chaotic but not to degree that it makes the game meaningless.

RATING: 10
This game works great as a gateway game with adults.  The rules are simple and quickly explained.  Everyone I have played this with really enjoyed it.  I have introduced it to co-workers and my gaming group with excellent results.

My 9 year old daughter enjoys playing this game.  My 7 year old is a bit reluctant to play.  But, this is a game even my wife enjoys on occassion.

The bits are fine.  I did replace the cloth bag with a larger one.  The placement of the pieces can be a bit tricky because the board is not groved at all.  But all that is very minor and does not detract from game play.

Plays just as well with 2, 3, 4 or teams.  I like it best with teams or 2.

--June '06

RATING: N/A
It was the 1st meeting of the Ark-La-Tex Gamers Association at Barnes & Noble Bookstore.  The rest of the gang hadn't arrived yet and some boys were talking about trying to find a game to play.  I suggested that they join me for a game of Ingenious.  Well, they all turned me down except one of the youngest ones.  He and I played a 2 player game and as we began to play not only his brothers and cousins started helping him learn the game but, his mother became interested as well.  By the time that he and I had finished our game the rest of the family wanted to play.  So, we played a 2nd game, only this time it was my young apponent and one of his cousins or brothers as one team, two of the other boys as a 2nd team, his parents as a 3rd team and myself and even though I won my first game one of the boys teams managed to win the 2nd game creating quite a bit of interest in the game of Ingenious.Soon after that Chris, one of our members arrived and he and I engaged in a 2 player game.  Chris had also played Ingenious before but I managed to beat him just the same.

RATING: 10
So simple.  This is a classic 'Why didn't I think of that first' type game.  Of course Reiner spends more time thinking of games than any one of the rest of us have waking hours, so I guess it was always likely that he would be the one to 'discover' this game.  The world is a better place for the good doctor's existence.  This has now even surpassed Lost Cities as my favourite 'small' Knizia game.

A very small moan.  You can get stuffed if you can't get one particular colour of tile.  Yes, you can replenish if you don't have that colour, but that doesn't guarantee you get the colour you want.  May introduce a house rule to allow further refreshing if you still don't pick the required colour.

RATING: 8
Very simple game with domino like pieces.  Players take turns laying down colored dominoes that are used to accumulate points of a certain color where lines are made.  Because it's a Reiner Knizia game the lowest color that you score in is your overall score.  So you really have to manage your scoring well.  This also allows other players to try and block off the one color that you need.  Very fun and quick game.

Abstract that reminded me of Exago in layout with Tigris & Euphrates scoring mechanism. Clever abstract that can get very bogged down with AP. Timer is essential but arguably a minute per turn is still way too much.

Time passes and further games don't really reveal much beneath the surface of this one. There are standard tactics to prevent opponants scoring unnecessarily but big scores will come from drawing the right tiles. Not a very challenging experience, down graded to a 6 and put on my trade list. Sold May 2014.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
People who hate abstract games will love this one. Even after they realize it doesn't get any higher on the abstract scale. They will walk round and say things as "hey, I love this, and I don't even like abstracts". And buy it for all their friends at the first opportunity. Yes, it's a Knizia that doesn't even pretend to have a theme. And suddenly we don't have to bicker about the gloss and chrome and judge it for what it is. And conclude it does exactly what it says on the tin.

The general idea of incremental scoring as more tiles are added to the sequence is certainly not a new one. "Domikant" and "Blokus" are only the first two of a long list of games I have played that immediately jump up and shout for attention. I am sure that there are game-magazines the world over that have presented cut-out games for their readership based on a similar principle. Yet somehow they weren't loud or convincing enough, as none of those similar-ish games gave me this instant way-up-there feeling. Was ditching the square tiles for hexagonal ones really all it took?

In all likelihood it is the first commandment on the rules-tablet that the good Doktor has given to the gaming world, that is the real reason why this game stands out from a colourful crowd: "your end score is the score for the element you are doing worst in". Suddenly attack and defence, and a natural necessity to balance your own play all become integral part of each strategy, triggering a whole new layer of plausible counter-moves to each situation. Handing people the teasing option to trigger bonus-moves for reaching the end of the scoring track in each colour is another simple way to add new layers of complexity with a simple concept, and possibly also a deadly trap for people who forget it's the bottom end on your plank that matters most and overdose on it.    

I yet have to find a flaw with this one. It is hellishly addictive, plays fast and exceptionally well with 2,3 and 4 players, both solo and in teams. People will grasp the rules (does that have an "s" at the end?) instinctively and there are a billion gazillion ways to get the tiles on the board and win. Even more to lose. There is an online version which has people up at all hours of the day and night, willing to give it a go. And Kosmos even made a shock-resistant travel edition. Another highly attractive feature is that the general gaming ability levels between players don't seem to matter as much, as people who normally shun stronger opponents quite happily sit down against normally intimidating opposition, forgetting they are eagerly sitting down for what was firmly defined as their worst gaming nightmare. Face it, if it could make breakfast I would marry it.

The only thing that could possibly improve it, is barring a certain BrettSpielWelt player from Thailand aggressively interfering with his team mate's moves and concentrate on his own placement.

I am curious if in time this will become another 10 in my list. For that it will need to keep me under its spell for quite a while longer. I suspect it will keep us easily engrossed for as long as it takes. It is, in one word, ... (resist, resist, you-know-better, no, darn, I caved)... Ingenious!

UPDATE: after a few times with 3 players I have changed my mind about suitability for 3, and decided not to play it that way any more. It seems that in a 3-player game too often 1 player gets shut out, usually because the other two have compatible opening hands, which seems to result in a tandem runaway team against one player, whose weakest colour is too easy to shut out. As a 2 or 4 player game it still takes some beating! One of the few games that is a steady climber on my list (8.5>9>9.5). The 3-player problem would normally slash it to a 9 tops, but few games are as intuitive to play as this, for gamers and non-gamers alike, simultaneously. And that more than makes up for its only flaw.

RATING: 8
I like this game, I enjoy this game...  but it's flawed a bit.  

Quite often you're just playing the hand you're dealt as best as you can play it that turn.  In doing so, often your choices are made for you, or you're just making the best of a bad situation more than any long-term strategy or free-form decision making.  The game is playing you.

I hesitate to say "it's all luck", skill will still win-out most of the time, but instead of a multi-player abstractish sort of game...it ends-up being sort of single-player abstractish sort of game.  Because you're all, within a tolerance, getting the same amount of crap per good...  it's just the play experience is one of deciding how to make the best of a situation with a super super limited amount of information available to you.  Your hand of 6 usually has 2-3 pieces that aren't even worth looking at, you don't know what your draws are in the future, and you don't know what the other players are even likely to do.   Just slap it down and hope everything works out for you.

So what I'm saying is that this ends up being kind of 'an exercise' more than a game...but without the flavor needed to turn into 'an experience'.

------------------------

Against the computer pro-tip:  The computer calculates "making the most of a move", so if you shut him down on his lowest value early in the game, he never bothers to remedy that situation.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is Knizia at his best, in fact I wish he would design more completely abstract games like this.  The game is utterly simple, you just play a tile and score for the colors it matches up with.  The "ingenious" part of the game is that you score each color separately, and your final score is equal to the lowest of your 6 colors.  This forces players to concentrate on diversifying and working in multiple areas at the same time.

There are lots of ways you can play this game.  You can be very competitive and block your opponents at every moment, or you can be semi-cooperative and help expand the color areas hoping that you'll be the one to get the biggest benefit.  There are many opportunities for clever play as well, and you can try multiple strategies to attain victory.  And since all scoring information is open you know where your opponents want to play and can play a very defensive game as well.

RATING: 8
[got in trade for Swarm]
I rarely choose abstracts over other games, but I usually end up enjoying them once I'm playing. This one was just right length (less than an hour), and it was fun. You can play for your own benefit or play to block others. I missed something at the end that cost me the game (and I ended up in last place), but I'll be more careful next time now that I'm aware of the possibility of having all possible scoring locations to a color blocked. The scoring is just like in Tigris and Euphrates, and I played the game the way I do that one, always making a move to score my lowest color. It works for me in T&E, which I almost always win, but it didn't work so well here. I guess it's better to make a high-scoring move on a color you're already strong in, and then you don't have to worry about that color anymore, or you can get it to 18 and get an extra move (I did that twice). I'll play it differently next time.

UPDATE: I actually chose this to play my second time (I don't usually choose abstracts). With 3 players it went pretty fast and made a nice filler. I did much better this time, too, not passing up big-point moves just so I can increase my lowest colors. That works in T&E, but not here. Getting to place the extra tile is a big advantage, giving you more chances at points, as well as enabling you to set up a two-part move.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Ingenious is not strictly my sort of game, since I like theme in general (with the notable exception of the GIPF series). But Ingenious is so undeniably clever, and has such an obvious draw for gamers of almost all stripes, and is also prototypically Knizian in accomplishing so much with so little, it's a game that still sucked me in.

Like many Knizia games, it's fundamentally a risk management game with a heavy dose of tactics. You need to look at the whole board and judge how "available" each color will be going forward, whether you need to jump on a bandwagon now or whether you can wait a little bit while you score smaller numbers of points in colors that may be harder to get in the future.

There really is a lot going on here for such a simple game, and it is very highly recommended for almost anyone.

When playing with 4, I much prefer the team variant to the straight 4-player. The loss of control with 4 individual players seems to hurt a little, but with two teams, you're back in business.

RATING: 6
Extremely simple to teach and play. Plays fast and has some decision making. A couple of problems. First is that luck of the draw can absolutely screw you, and though the tile replacement rule helps, it seems to not help as much as you would think. 

The other issue is that the writing is often on the wall as to who will win at most 2/3 of the way through the game. Sometimes, with the right tiles, you can come back through an "ingenious" double tile play, but not often. I actually think the early game is far more interesting in how you block off early popular colors and set yourself up for that end game. 

Overall, for the play time and the fact that it can easily be played with anyone, pretty nice.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Never say it's kind of Dominoes (but not really!) as it will turn many people off... It's a pretty good abstract game which scales perfectly well from 2-4 players. The scoring mechanism, taken from Tigris & Euphrates and other games, is great and adds tension because you might have scored max points in a lot of categories, but if your lowest score is e.g. 2, your final score will be 2... 

Sure, you can get screwed this way, but you can also screw others! And if you don't have the tiles of your lowest score, you can switch them with new ones which mitigates the luck of the draw. I think it's yet a pretty good game made by Knizia - some might even say it's an ingenious design... (Sorry for the terrible pun!)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
My criterion for rating games


http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/147053/item/2300390#item2300390


1) Sense of wonder: Not much difference from game to game. This is why abstracts are rated so lowly by me. Not enough variation from game to game.

2) Meaningful/fun decisions. Lots of decisions on where to place tiles to score ingenious, but also to keep low colors moving up.

3) Player interaction: A good amount of interaction as players look to build on what their opponent's play to build large rows off of them and also prevent them from getting their low colors up in score.

4) Components/artwork: Good components for an abstract. Pretty lousy artwork, but I enjoy the components.

5) Game length/complexity: Perfect time and complexity for what it is. Can knock out several games in an hour with a party game crowd.

RATING: 7
Ingenious is an abstract game that my wife and I have played to death! With more than 70 plays, I can safely say "we got our money's worth."

With Ingenious, players are in turn placing double-hex-shaped pieces in six different colors, scoring based on how many of the matching color are in proximity. Players track their progress in the six colors, but the winner is the player who does best in their worst color, which forces players to diversify.

To be honest, the reason we played this game so much is because my wife was reluctant to try new titles, which is no longer the case. But it's interesting to consider a game that we've played with such frequency. I can honestly say that Ingenious has held up fairly well over such repeat play, as my wife and I learned how to study each other's board, block each other and marginalize scoring opportunities.

One other perk to consider is that Ingenious allows for team play, which I always enjoy. As long as folks are open to an abstract game, this is very solid family-weight gaming.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Nicknamed "the mold-growing game" among my friends for the colorful organic patterns it produces, Ingenious is quite delightful. It has two central flaws, neither of which really impede the fun of playing it, but both of which reduce its overall strategic complexity. First, the rules as stated clearly intend for players to grow their scores in a balanced way, making sure not to focus on any one color too much compared to the others - but in reality, getting one Ingenious by filling a color track up typically makes it a lot easier to get more, leading to a snowball effect that rewards acting against the intent of the rules. Second, despite the name, this game is actually very easy if you have a good grasp of how scores are counted, since there is almost always an optimal move, and it's generally pretty obvious what that move is.

All in all, a very good game with a little bit of pretentiousness in it's theming that overshadows some of its gameplay strengths.

RATING: 7
Basically take a Euphrat style scoring system, and then graft it onto a pure abstract tile laying game that rewards getting long rows of a given colour while simultaneously shutting off your opponents from the same colour when at all possible. Of the Knizia tile laying games that I've played I would rank this only behind Euphrat (Euphrat is just much deeper with its conflict rules) but certainly in front of both Durch and Samurai. This is just as accessible as Durch, but more interesting because of the need to go after different types of points continuously instead of just grabbing the most obvious big score that is left on the board.

All of that said, I had a copy of this but then got rid of it because it was nothing I was ever volunteering for the table. With 3-4 players I would rather play Euphrat, with 2 there are a plethora of fine abstract options (i.e.: Insert GIPF project game here).

RATING: 5
Genius is a simple tile-laying game with a curious option to play it against yourself. The scoring mechanism seems straightforward, but you have to take into account that if you score a lot of points, a player after you might score even more. Luck seems to have a large influence despite the rule which allows you to exchange the tiles in your hand for new ones, but the game is over quickly enough for it to be too big of a problem. It plays smoothly with any number of players and has a pleasing one-more-go appeal, but it misses character. Not a bad abstract, but not spectacular either.

[b]Update[/b] The game gets better if players become more creative in laying down blocking tiles, thus hindring subsequent 'leeching'. Still not a great fan of it, though, as it lacks agression.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.4
This fiercely addictive game is so easy to learn and plays in a short amount of time.  Perfect for families of all ages.

Players place 2-part tiles which feature symbols in 6 colors.  You score points for each color the new tile touches that matches, as far out in a straight line as possible, and in every direction.  The twist?  Your final score is for whichever color has the lowest value at game's end - so you have to spread out across the board.

Great little game that I'd play anytime.

RATING: 6
Components: Sturdy plastic tiles, and a simple yet attractive board. The patterns could be more appealing, but they are at least functional. The travel edition is too small.

Experience: Very simple game, but with opportunity to play both defensively and offensively. I don't see much long-term strategy in this: I score the most points I can each turn (rather than sacrificing points on one turn to set up for a big score on a subsequent turn). It's fun trying to manage all six colors, caring about the stragglers, but also trying to max-out a color for the extra turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Loads to think about and little strategies to employ: it's beneficial to play an identical tile after one already done and so using a tile you have two of in a starting hand seems sensible.

Checking your opponents' score gives you an idea of which shapes you should block of and which are fine to leave open. And which you need to catch up on.

In the end-game, playing is mainly a matter of scoring as many points as possible but about 1/3 of the way in, there's enough of a 'spread' in the players' points to inform some strategies relating to their relative value.

You need to consider the spaces available and the option of 'killing' a space by only leaving lone hexagons, as well as the 'ingenious' move.

The random elements keep the game relatively light and exciting.

I could imagine enjoying playing this over and over for hours and hours.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Score points on 6 differently coloured tracks by placing double hexagon tiles on a hexagonal game board. The points are counted along the radius of the 5 free sides of each one of the 2 hexagons of the double hexagon tile you place. The points are equal to the number of hexagons showing the same colour/symbol in a straight line from the hexagon you placed. Your final score is your worst score among the 6 differently coloured tracks.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Take Knizia's beloved maxmin scoring mechanism and distil it into its purest form: remove any pasted on theme, any dynamic element (such as battles in Tigris & Euphrates), any diversely valued tile (such in Samurai). Add a costless mechanism to try and even out the (un)luck of the draw. Throw in a couple of variants (solitaire and  4 players/2 teams) for added value. 
There you have Ingenious, a fun, fast, simple yet deep sort of "geometrical scrabble" heavy-filler game, which scales pretty well 2-4.

Maybe not enough complex to reach the god/demigod status, yet as close to it as it is possible.

(N players: 3>4=2, when played with standard rules. Very good when using both the solo and 2 teams variants)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This abstract uses the scoring system from Euphrates & Tigris, as an entire game, basically. The player selects a tile from their "scrabble tray" which displays a pair of colored symbols and then places it, on the hex board, adjacent to a color that matches one of the pair. Scoring is based on the matching symbols that surround it. Players are scored at the end, based on their lowest scoring color. The player that ranks the highest, wins. Very simple to teach and play.

RATING: 8
Rating up a notch from 8.  Elegant abstract tile laying game.  Players alternate playing domino-like double-hexagon tiles onto a playing area.  Each tile contains one or two symbols which score points for lines of matching symbols off the five open hexsides at each end of the tile.  There are six different symbols to worry about.  Final score is the points earned in the lowest symbol and highest "lowest" wins.  Excellent game that doesn't tax the brain too much due to limited hand size and very clear board layout.  Playes well with two, three or four players.  Recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Probably one of the most accessible abstract strategy games. You simply place a tile and count up adjacent hexes of the same color in a straight line. The winning condition is also neat in that a player's lowest score in a particular color is their final score and whoever has the highest score wins. This game  works well regardless of the number of players you have available (it can even be played solo). A good gateway game by Reiner Knizia.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Ingenious is one of the best abstract games out there, bar none. It scales completely well from 2 to 4 players; and although tile draws are random, the ability to swap out your hand if you're missing your lowest color minimizes the luck aspect. In the early game Ingenious has quite a leisurely feel as everyone tries to set up unbroken chains of colors. Often players will even work together to get an Ingenious in one or two colors. 

But as the board fills up, the game suddenly turns much more cutthroat. Since your score in Ingenious is whatever your lowest color is, you can't afford to focus on one or two (or even three) colors to the detriment of others. And you'll quickly find that if one of your colors is trailing, it can be rather difficult to catch up as your opponents quickly cut off your options. 

The end game is where Ingenious really shines, however. It's a nail-biting experience to be only one or two points ahead of your opponent, trying to figure out how to prevent him from leapfrogging you on the scoring board. Do you play non-connecting colors to deny him points? Or do you try to squeeze a few more points out of your trailing color? Ingenious always gives you lots of options, but you have to be flexible and willing to change gears on the fly if you're going to pull out a win. I love this game.

[b]Update[/b]: Increased rating from an 8 to a 9. I've owned this game for several years, and I never get tired of playing it. Its ability to play equally as well with 2 players as with 3 or 4 puts it in a rare category on BGG.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
I wanted to like this, since I’m a fan of abstracts, but sadly it is a poor game. It has two significant flaws:

1. Optimal strategy is relatively easy to see – you simply choose tiles that match your lowest scoring colours, and it is obvious where on the board to place them to gain the most points. It’s like assembling a jigsaw with references on the pieces to tell you where they go. This in itself is dull, but the shallowness of the strategy is badly affected by the tile drawing, which is random. I suspect that most people who play this game play more or less optimal moves (it’s that simple), meaning that victory or defeat is determined by whether you are lucky enough to draw the correct colour. It is entirely possible to go three, four or five rounds without drawing the correct colour, resulting in a loss that was entirely out of your control. This encroachment of a randomising mechanic in to an abstract game renders it deeply tedious. In one game I played, I had all colours but red on the final rank, with red at nine points because I didn’t draw any red tiles. 
2. As with many abstract games that are more than two players (army of frogs is another offender), it has terrible downtime. If you play with someone who neglects to plan ahead when other people are playing their moves, the whole game comes to a tedious, grinding halt while they sit there on their turn and deliberate over which move to play. This is why abstract games should really only be two player games. 

The game also has none of the qualities I expect from a good abstract – flat strategy, no tactical interaction, non-deterministic victory conditions. 

Dull and frustrating. 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[Tile-laying, Abstract] [4P:6, 4P Partnership:6, 3P:7, 2P:7] Reiner doesn't do abstracts much, but when he does them he does them right. Ingenious is a rather addictive tile-laying game that takes the balanced-scoring mechanism from Euphrat & Tigris and grafts it onto a line-of-symbols-forming system that's surprisingly simple and yet gives more meat to chew on than meets the eye. Toss in a couple of simple rules (a hand dump, plus yelling "Ingenious!" with gusto) and you've got a good middleweight game that's easy to teach and is very accessible. The only thing it really lacks is drama (and it doesn't have the strong tension of the better RK games) which will hurt it in the long run, but I don't think Reiner was explicitly going for that here. I'm thinking he wanted a relaxing, family-oriented casual game, and he nailed it nicely despite the occasional feeling that the game is overstaying its welcome when playing 4P with players take several minutes to move. Fantasy Flight's top-notch production values, from the plastic tiles, large board and Scrabble-style tile racks make this a damned good package. If you have space on your shelf for an abstract, this is as good a choice for up to 4 players as you can get.

RATING: 9
I love this game.  It has a domino-esque simplicity, with strategy out the wazoo.  You get points for making lines of the same color which radiate from dual-hexagon-shaped pieces you place each turn.  The longer the line, the more points.  The twist:  Your score is equal to the color of which you have the LEAST.  So you are constantly trying to bring up your low scores.  It's really a blast to play, and beautiful to look at.  I only wish the board had raised hexagon shapes in the middle of each space to help hold the bits in place.

RATING: 8
Great family game. We tend to play this when the extended family come to visit. 
The pegs can be a little fiddly, especially with a new board.
The scoring scheme takes a couple of goes to get the hang of and is best learned by seeing a few examples.
Sometimes play with the house-rule that says a player can only retrieve one new tile from the bag per turn. That way a player who gets to the end of the track will have an extra go but will play the remainder of the game with one fewer tile.
Buy if you want a fun game that the whole family can play

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I played a lot of Ingenious solitaire when I first got it, trying to figure out how to play best. After many plays I discovered I was using the wrong rules, which is pretty embarrassing given that there aren't that many rules in the first place. I still play solitaire occasionally trying to figure out what the best strategy might be.

Multi-player is a very different game as you're trying not to set everybody else up. As far as I can tell you find one colour you've done well in and most other people haven't, and try to close that colour down. Closing a colour down is difficult as there can be many tentacles to score on and you can't do all of them at the same time as well as scoring for yourself. 

I find the game to be an interesting solitaire puzzle, but there's an awful lot of player chaos in a multi-player game and it doesn't really engage me. It is however easy to teach and so is a good game for newbies.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This is a very fun area majority type game, where players are trying to score all the different colors equally, because your lowest scoring color at game end is your total score. You gain points in the colors by lining up rows of the same colors. There's a little more to it than that, but that's pretty much the basics of the game.

The components are very well made and look great. Even the tile racks are very thick a sturdy.

This is a good family game and most gamers would enjoy a game or two as well.

Oh and there's evena solotaire varient, which is always a plus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Initial Rating: 5 -> 6

Ingenious is a tile-laying game of matching patterns and positioning your tiles in order to maximize your points, while still minimizing your opponents possible gain by blocking key tiles.

You score points in six different colors during the game, and every move you do gets you points in 0, 1 or 2 different colors. The amount of points you get for each tile depends on how large the color pattern was on the board before you put your tile there. Essentially, a big preexisting red pattern will get you lots of red points. If your tile is not adjacent to any preexisting pattern, you get no points.

The goal of the game is to collect sets of all six colors. This means that if you have 2 of one color and 10 in all the other five colors, you have only collected two complete sets.

In general you want to maximize the number of points you get for every tile you put on the board, but herein lies a trap. If you don't bite the bullet once in a while and skip the high points to score a few points in your weak colors, you will find that your opponent will block all access to that color for the rest of the game. For a beginner, this is the hard part of the game: Figuring out that you are losing by having too many points.

Because of how the scoring works, you generally want to play after your opponent because wherever he plays, there is now a good chance that he has made a good-scoring area into a great-scoring area. Of course, sometimes this is a feint, and you should try to find the area on the board that your opponent is trying to distract you from. 

Sometimes you will find yourself in a position where you really need a certain tile before that color is completely blocked off. But since you don't have it in hand, you distract your opponent somewhere else by giving him something shiny to look at, and hopefully you will draw the right tile for your next round.

This is a fairly light game, with a limited number of decisions. It has some small similarities to 'lost cities' where you often hold a number of tiles in your hand just to keep them from your opponent, or to wait for a better time to use them. There are some considerations on what color to score when, based on how many tiles has entered the games of a certain color and so on. But it is mostly a relaxing kind of game that you can play quick and breezy. The reliance on the tile draw is not insignificant. The better player usually wins as there are some subtle skills to learn, but there will be a number of games that come down to that you simply didn't draw enough of a color.

Now, as a two-player game I think I would rate this a bit lower, but as a four-player partnership game I find it strangely intriguing. For some reason, a game that I find merely ok because of lack of control, becomes more fun when you lose even more control :)

The partnership is very similar to the two-player game, but you play on bigger area and you have an ally that -in theory- will help you win.
How effective this partnership is depends of course on how good you can read each others moves, and anticipate what your partner wants you to do. This usually leads to some interesting post-game discussions, and for me this meta-game makes the original game more interesting as well. Ingenious also gains some points for being one of the few abstract strategy games to be able to play with 4 players.

RATING: 8
Einfach Genial is a good choice for players who don’t mind trying an abstract placement game, but who usually find such games too dry or too intimidating or too intense.  Einfach Genial is none of those things.  The random tile draw and the multiplayer design put this more in the category of a German-style game than a typical “abstract.”  I would think of it as a German spatial placement game that simply lacks a real-world setting or theme.  For me, it scratches the same itch as Ta Yü.  Both are visually appealing, nontaxing, liesurely, and both nicely limit a player's choices to keep them from becoming overwhelmed.  I prefer Ta Yü with two players, and Einfach Genial with the four player partnership rules.

RATING: 8
Bought this one without ever having played it.  What can I say, I'm a Knizia fan... plus it has the balanced scoring rules that I liked about Tigris and Euphrates.

Blocking other players from scoring their low colours is very important in this game.  One of the interesting tradeoffs in this game is whether to block off a long line of a single colour so that the next player can't extend them for easy points, vs. letting it be extended so that you can extend it again in future (presumably because you need them).  Same for "blockading" a group of one colour by playing a completely unrelated tile against the edge of the region (to stop those easy 6-10 point plays that can arise if you don't).

Interesting rules questions: Can you place a tile in the middle of empty space, not connected to any other tiles?  I say yes.  Can you choose not to play a tile at all?  I say no.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
10-3-11
This is a nifty little game.  Actually, after playing an online version of it, I wasn't expecting the board and bits to be so large!

I really like this as a gateway game because it can quickly grab a new player but suck them in with the subtlety of strategy.  The open information on scoring allows for some blocking opportunities and screwage.

Speaking of which, I really like the scoring system.  Reminiscent of T&E, it punishes you for forgetting about a particular color.  Especially if you wait until late when the board starts to fill, your opportunities can dwindle quickly if you're not careful.  The 'ingenious' bonus plays are an interesting touch, but in my games so far, I haven't gotten to utilize them a whole lot.

The game is pretty quick to setup and play which is great.  There are a couple minor downsides for me though.  I haven't had the chance to play with more than 2 yet and fear that this will suffer from Wasabi syndrome - where the board is so different from your last turn, it is difficult to formulate any plans or strategy.  I also think it might be like Blokus for me - fun for a while, but not the greatest in the replayability department.

For now though, a very fun game that I'm happy to have!

RATING: 8
Nice, clever and quick game that makes everyone feel like they can do well at it right from the off, thanks to it's deceptive simplicity. The customary 'clever' Knizia scoring mechanism here is a nice one, with players scoring their lowest colour total, but getting a free turn every time they max out a colour, which leads to some interesting strategies and play decisions.

The 'scrabble with colours' feel means this is an easy shoe-in as a gateway game, but it can feel a little dry sometimes. 

Another Knizia 'so elegantly simple I wish I'd thought of it' gem, then. :)

RATING: 4
2016-04-05

Initial Rating: 6.0 (October 2010)

It’s been years since I last played Ingenious. I don’t really like the game and I never did. I played the game for the first time on 2010, a year I liked most games I played, so if I rated it a 6.0 I didn’t like it very much. 

Its decisions aren’t as hard as some friends seem to think and I feel it has a lot if uncontrollable luck, furthermore with the full complement of players. I prefer to play it with two so the travel edition is currently in my game collection (because my SO enjoys the game). The rules are very easy to explain, so Ingenious is perfect for casual play or to introduce non gamers to our beloved hobby.

Bottom line, Ingenious is way too light for my current tastes, it also has too much luck. I generally enjoy more difficult abstracts. I don’t feel any urge to play the game again.

Current Rating: 4.0

RATING: 8
Really growing on me. It's almost a bit of Tigris and Euphrates light.

Really solid game, pretty damn good and will be around for me a long time.

Even the solo play is good.

This game has surpassed blockus for me. I'm really enjoying Ingenious. It's a great game for scrabble players and the family. Very easy to pick up, some real tactics, some strategy availible, symools and colors on the bits to help color blind folk still be able to play unhindered, very nice game.

A true hit for me and I still, really, see Tigris and Euphrates in this; granted not as themed nor as deep, but the lack of theme makes this game really good for everyone actually.

Is it exciting and laugh out loud glorious mayhem? Nope, but I tell you this game really works and will be around on my table for decades if I can make it last. Very easy to pick up play, very easy to understand scoring, very scalable, and no geek theme to chase nongamers away. Tell 'em it's 'kind of' like scrabble and pull them in.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The epitome of an accessible but absorbing abstract game, good for gamers and casual players alike. On your turn, you lay a tile with two colored spaces on it, and score points in those colors based on the size and shape of the groupings formed by your placement. You cannot neglect any of the six colors, because at the end of the game, your weakest color is the only one that matters (this is the much touted "highest lowest" Knizia scoring system). Simple, approachable, and, by all accounts, deep as well. I enjoyed it and look forward to the occasional play in the future.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
quick playing, light strategy game
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is one of those games with simple rules, but lots of strategy. Colored double hex tiles are played to maximize scoring. Scoring occurs in six different colors, but your lowest of the six is your score at the game end. When you place a tile, for each newly formed linear sequence, the number of hexes are counted and then the matching 1 or 2 newly placed hexes are subtracted. This takes a few turns to get used to and with all the options this requires a lot of calculations by game end. Still, this was a fun game.

RATING: 7

RATING: 5
This is a fun abstract that has a Tigris & Euphrates scoring mechanism. You score points in 6 colors and then your final score is your lowest score of the 6 colors. On any given play you can choose from 6 tiles to place, so there are a lot of options each turn. I haven't figured out yet if there is a viable strategy that focuses on blocking opponents rather than scoring yourself. I also have a feeling that the "luck factor" can take over if someone can draw a higher than normal number of the same-color tiles. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Interesting game in several ways. 

I'm definitely not a fan of the light plastic pieces and the rather unattractive symbols, and I recognise that this purely cosmetic aspect of the game gives me an attitude to the game. Yes, I'm that shallow. But just as a game of Go with cheap plastic bits and a thin card board somehow doesn't have the gravitas of a version of the game with heavy ceramic pieces and a wooden board, so Ingenious signals, for me, light and rather superficial.

Which is isn't. Yes, it's easy to explain the rules, and easy to start playing, and easy to understand the scoring, but after a few turns the game becomes tactically more and more demanding. 

There's enough chance in the tile draw to make it possible for any player to win any given game, though a skilled player will win in the long run. But will there ever be a long run? Each game is a bit too dominated by chance for my taste, and makes it one of those games I'm never all that keen to get to the table - except to introduce someone to a game that isn't Scrabble.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I am just such a fan of T&E style scoring in games.  By itself provides such an elegant twist.  Since your score is based upon which color you scored lowest in the better player will try to place in such as way as to limit opponents.  Additionally some well timed "Ingenious" plays near the end of the game can help launch you to victory with bonus moves.  In many cases you can also manipulate for a full discard.  Occasionally randomness will still bite you. Still uncertain therefore if I like it better than something like Quirkle even if I do highly prefer the scoring here. Here you can win with 11 points rather than 211 elsewhere.  No need for pencils or calculators either!

RATING: 6
Another family-friendly game from Reiner Knizia. The goal is to score points in each of six colors, by lining up hexagonal tiles radiating outward from the tile that you play. The trick is to do this without setting up your opponents for an even bigger score. Easy to learn, and fairly well done (though a board that keeps pieces from sliding around would be even better). My problem with this game (and this could be said of nearly every Knizia game) is that it feels like a math exercise more than a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A tile laying game where you score in six different colors by aligning your pieces with existing rows of matching color. As more points are scored in a given color it can open up windows of opportunity to score even more in that color but you have to balance those opportunities against your need for scoring in other colors because, in the end, your final score is only as good as your worst color. 

This is a clever game that is easy to learn and does have some luck of the draw but also really rewards strategic play. I really enjoy the tension of balancing trying not to neglect any color while trying to benefit from as many high scoring opportunities as possible.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Ingenious is a clever abstract game that can work charm as an introductory game for certain people. The simplicity of their rules along with its polished look can grab the attention of those who consider that all non-classic and socially accepted board and card games are a pastime for the kids.

Initially, the rules might seem clunky and unnecessarily complicated but they are very simple to explain if you see them in action. This means that the best way to explain a game is either see people playing it or laying out several play examples. This way, you can teach the rules in a few minutes, which is a huge plus. The basic core is as follows: each player has a hand of two-coloured tiles. In your turn you play a tile adjacent to a previously placed one and score points in one or both of its colours depending on the surrounding tiles. By the end of the game, each player's score is his lowest scored colour (that is, is someone has 16, 17, 18, 20 and 20 has a score of 16) and the winner is the one with the highest score.

Personally, I don't like it that much. The interaction is very indirect and, even if play is simple, is not that easy to see how your movements are going to affect other players. This leads to a very friendly and family oriented environment of which I'm not particularly fond of. Yes, you can block other player's scores but its very hard to win if you focus on an aggressive strategy. And this friendliness is what doesn't convince me, as I would have much preferred a more conflict-oriented approach.

If you are looking for an introductory abstract that works well with two to four players this is one of your best options, if not the best. But if you like conflict-oriented games with direct interaction you should stay away from this one, as it's the total opposite.

Rating: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:

:thumbsdown:1

RATING: 9
I know abstract games aren't as popular as those with themes but honestly, this is worth giving a try. Unlike Blockus this one has the ability to block, lead, mislead, and completely mess with your opponents while at the same time defending your own position. The random tile draw adds some unassurance of future moves but since you have six tiles the randomness isn't THAT random and planning can still happen (similar to planning in Scrabble). Great game that I'm scared may get overlooked simply because it uses colors and shapes rather than space men or knights. Oh, and for the color blind, the game is not dependent on colors to play--I was proud of the designers to make both shape and color dependent upon each other so that color was a non-issue.

RATING: 7.5
In Ingenious, a.k.a. Einfach Genial, players take turns placing colored domino-style tiles on a game board, scoring for each color group that they enlarge. The trick, however, is that a player's score is equal to her worst-scoring color, not her best, so she needs to score for all colors instead of specializing in only one or two.

RATING: 6
First, I want to say that I hate almost all knizia games. Apparently, it's common to have auction in Knizia games, ingenious does hot have any. Which is a good thing, else I would have rated it like 3 or below.

It's a simple abstract game where place tiles to make lines of color to score more points. Each color has it's own scoring track. If I remember correctly, you score the lowest track at the end of the game, So you want to make sure to boost up all your track. If you fill up a track, you get an additional move. So there is an opposition between the need to concentrate and diversify the colors you score.

The game is not so bad, but it's really not the kind of game you would play 3 times in a row. Twice per year would be fair enough.

RATING: 8
Surpasses Blokus for the crown of popular light-middleweight multi-player abstract game (By popular, I clumsily mean a game which has broken out of local game stores and can be found in a variety of retailers).

All the surrounding comments give a good flavour of the game but what strikes me is the rules; or to be exact what is not said in the rules.  They are beautifully succinct with excellent examples but this is a multi-player abstract where the true beauty of the game is discovered between the lines of the rules.  This is not a treasure that you are led to and shown but you have to discover yourself.

I understand that this is not a rare occurance, nearly all games ... the good ones at least ... have this self-discovery element, those little eureka moments, but I feel the excellent rules are a great example and allow this game to breathe and develop a life of its own (A clue to its high replay value and ability to be played by all levels of gamer).  A credit to all involved in developing this wonderful game.

RATING: 7
Reiner's abstract version of his game where your score it the score of the lowest of six tracks.  Place a double hex tile and score the two colors adjacent to it in a straight line from the hex placed.  There is a lot of strategy because you can place and cutoff other players.  It is a simple yet highly strategic.  I find the game very fun and one of his best.

RATING: 6
On the mechanical design, there's nothing to complain about.

But the physical design of this version is not okay. 
The tiles are passable, but since the groove on the back is not used to hold the tiles on the board, they should have been equally thick all over. The heavier the better.
The board is dull and there should have been embossed centers to hold the tiles like in the travel edition (this would require a deeper groove on the back of the tiles), or holes and corresponding pokes on the tiles.
The scoreboards are the worst. They look way dull and it is far too easy to accidentally push a marker with your sleeve. It should have been pegboards, sliders or something else that works.

I know that all of the above raises the production price, but abstract games simply must have better physical design than normal games.

RATING: N/A
Jeder Spieler hat sechs dominosteinähnliche Plättchen, auf denen zwei von sechs möglichen Symbolen (Farben) abgebildet sind. Wer an der Reihe ist, versucht, die Plättchen so auf das Spielbrett zu legen, dass er möglichst viele Punkte dafür bekommt und zieht anschließend ein Plättchen nach. Punkte bekommt man für jedes gleiche, bereits ausliegende Symbol in unmittelbarer Folge, ausgehend von den Plättchenkanten. Die Punkte werden auf einer Zählleiste abgetragen. Erreicht man dort 18 Punkte, darf man einen Bonus-Zug machen.

RATING: 8.5
I think the whole game here is its "genius" scoring system. The winner is declared after comparing the worst colour in points of each player when the whole board is covered with tiles. Thus, we are forced to try to evenly deploy all the kinds of the coloured tiles.

For some reason, presumably because of the whole setting, it reminds me of scrabble, but "ingenious" is much, much better.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In [i]Ingenious[/i], you need to constantly evaluate your needs relative to those of your opponent.  Sometimes your best move is to maximize points without regard for blocking, whereas at other times going for the block, even if you don't score any points, is best.

The player who wins is typically the first to identify his opponent's primary weakness, which is often not as simple as seeing which color is lagging behind in points.  It is a combination of your score in a color, your opponent's score in that color, and the number of openings that color has on the board and how easy they are to close off.

This is Knizia's best two-player game. As a 3-4 player game it loses a bit of luster. With two players, I rate it a perfect 10.

RATING: 5.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Game in a Nutshell: Lay tiles making lines of similar colors. Score points depending on how many consecutive similar colors there are in that line. Keep all six of your different colors scores high because the final score is what your lowest scoring color is.

Played as: 1,2,3,4 (solo and team)  player 

Pros: A quick, easy to learn game with enough luck that everyone, no matter what their skill-level, feels like they have a shot. Also very versatile in that it gets on the table in many different situations. Scales well from 2-4 players. My favorite part is when you get into the game, how much you begin to play off your opponent's scores bringing a higher level of interaction then appears at first.

Cons: Tile draws can hurt you and I guess it's annoying to play with table bumpers / heavy tile nudgers. Solitaire play is too fiddly and random to be worth its time.

Other: At first I thought of this as mini or light Tigris and Euphrates, but as a played each one more, I've found them to be excellent for their own unique qualities-- no longer feeling so similar.

Best Use: Similar to Carcassonne, fills in almost anywhere.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Early game is a mite boring and something about the components turn me off.  The intricacies of blocking in the late game are it's saving grace.

Caveat: I don't understand the game well. The best players play at an unbelievably high level, and Knizia, who strains to remain impartial about his games, let slip in an interview that it might be his favorite of his games. These two facts together suggest I haven't played enough or with the right people, and if I did I might see the game in a different, more favorable light.

[UPDATE] After playing it through the iphone app, my opinion of it has changed for the much better.  Just as is the case for many people who've rated it, my initial tepid impression has gotten hotter with better understanding.  As in Scrabble, rack management matters a lot but it takes a while to figure out how to do it. Now that I know some things about rack management, the whole game is much better from beginning to end, and accordingly I've upped my rating to an 8. Great game. 

[UPDATE] After even more time has passed (and about 1000 plays total), this game is just straight beautiful, at least for two players, which is how I play it. I now see why Knizia is so proud of it. This game sort of sums up his whole career, in a way: the complexity hidden inside unassuming simplicity and the signature scoring method, the profound balance, the exquisite implementation of chance. A lot of BGGers seem to complain about Knizia's turn toward simplicity in recent years. There seems to be a widespread assumption among BGGers that simple rules mean shallow or uninvolved play, which I'd guess comes from the BGGer habit of playing many games in a shallow way (thereby failing to see past the surface of most games). I've gone the other way: I think his turn towards simplicity has made him a better designer. Ingenious is a prime example.   

This is a game I'd love to play in a tournament setting. There aren't many games with chance events for which that's true. And yet at the same time I can play it casually with a non-gamer.  

One gripe: the components (of the FFG edition). The pieces feel ugly, and they don't look great on the also-not-wonderful board. I think it could have been done much better, and I think this weakness is one of the things holding the game back.

RATING: 7
[microbadge=1714]  [microbadge=21390]

[u]01/24/2013[/u]: This is my favorite abstract game.  As with any good abstract, the rules are simple and yet there is a lot to this game due to the scoring.  You score points based on the color tiles you play throughout the game keeping each color separate.  At the end of the game, your score is the color with the least amount of points.  So, even though you scored 18, 18, 15, 12, 10, and 7.  Your final score is only 7.  That little caveat makes this game very interesting to play, as you must look for scoring big points in all colors while blocking your opponent from getting what they need.

A bonus with this game is the scalability.  You can play 2 to 4 players on the same board using more or less of the area depending on the number.  The components in my edition (Fantasy Flight red box) are okay but not great.  The board is slightly warped, and the tiles could be a little thicker with painted designs instead of stickers.  But overall this is a great game that should work with almost any audience.

[size=10][u][i][b]r{Initial Thoughts}r (after 5 plays):[/u][/b][/i][/size] 
* Excellent tile-laying abstract, with typical "best-of-the-worst" Knizia scoring
* Easy to teach (but not to master) and lends itself to "best-x-out-of-x" series
* Good for abstract/puzzle fans and teaching colors, patterns and basic arithmetic
* Initial rating: [b]9/10[/b]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is an abstract game wherein players get points by placing colored tiles so that they create the longest runs of those colors.  There are six different colors in all, and points are tracked for each color separately (so a run of four yellow pieces generates 4 yellow points, but no points for any other color). Victory is determined by the points each player has in their lowest-point-total color, so players must be sure to place pieces of every color.  

Tiles are drawn randomly, so the board has a different layout each game.  This game is a great short game (we finished two-player games in less than 30 minutes).

RATING: 7.5
Knizia abstract game. Two joined hexagonal pieces are the playing pieces. Each piece has two symbols on it, out of 6 possible symbols. Play on the map to score points by linking up with other like symbols. Like Euphrates and Tigres, score is the minimum of all your symbol points. This is a delightful abstract game, can be played with all kinds of people, and it is also fun when played in teams.

RATING: 6
A really well designed game.  It can be taught in almost no time and is easy to play from the first game, but still requires brainpower to plan your strategy.  The mechanic of having your overall score being the score of your weakest colour makes the game special -- it will be easy to grab points in several colours, but much harder to make sure that you don't end up leaving one underdeveloped.  There are also opportunities for screwage by blocking off the colours your opponents need to score in, and this can work really well if you're careful to plan when you'll get your bonus plays.

RATING: 8
A good abstract game. The trick is finding plays that will get you points, but not set up the next played to get even more. Still not the greatest at this, but it seems like a good strategy is to block what is needed by your opponent. It's always important to be aware of how you compare to your opponent in each color. If you can block off a color that your opponent is weak in, you can concentrate on collecting everything else, and hope to keep them weak in that color.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
For Ingenious Rainer Knizia uses only a couple of features from his masterpiece Euphrat & Tigris (scoring, placing tiles next to matching colours), leading to a less complex but more refined and ultimately more rewarding game. Drawing a tile every turn there is obviously some luck, but the more plays, the more ways are found to channel it: being this a game where wins are earned by constant scoring and with lots of free board until the end the winner is always the best player.

As most abstracts, this game works at best with two players, when there's more time and space to unfold. Besides, there's a good solitaire in it as well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Interesting game with a really good scoring mechanism that is very similar to the manner in which the winner is dtermined in Tigris and Euphrates (i.e. your lowest scoring color IS your score and hence whoever has the highest "low" score wins).  The scoring emphasizes balance, which seems to be a theme in many Knizia games.  There is also a point in the game when you need to switch your focus to defense and block opponents' scoring.  Finding that point can be tough.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A terrific play-with-spouse game. Easily works as a gateway game. Follows the Knizia model of scoring your "lowest highest". That is, in this game you score colors, and at the game, you look to see who scored the lowest in any color. That person comes in last. Ties are resolved by seeking the next lowest up. The winner is the one who's lowest score in any color was the "highest low". This forces you to constantly rebalance your effort as you play, for even though you are scoring well in a few colors, it's the last color's advance that will be tallied in the end.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Update: As my playing time is greatly reduced I feel any free playtime should be spent playing the more climatic predecessor Euphrat and Tigris

The victory condition of "weakest value scores" used in E&T has now expanded to 6 colours and made public. And this last change highlights the importance of visual status for better gaming experiences, elevating the game to dramatic swings in who's winning at the moment and can instill a competitive tension as you (and your teammate) try to push your straggling colours, whilst limiting the progress of your opponents weakest colours. So there are tactical offensive & defensive tile placements & when to take advantage of extra turns. One of the few abstracts I enjoy, but it can get nasty and alittle humourless but poses interesting problems. My rating is for 4 player partners as this situation means you have a dependency on your partner playing well or setting you up and as a result situations can get incredibly tense. Good 2 player lunchtime game, rating 7; with 3 two opponents reduces alot of control.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This game is all about the scoring system -- it seems that the mechanics were almost an afterthought.  Still, I think it works.  There are many tough choices to be made, and it is very important to think both offensively and defensively.  If you can deny any particular color to your opponent(s), you'll probably win the game.

On the down side, the tile draw is a much bigger factor than in T&E, for example.  A few bad tile draws can really hose you, and unlike T&E, there is only one way to score points in this game.  My other minor complaint is with the board, which allows the pieces to slide around.  Some sort of locking mechanism for the pieces would have been a great improvement.

RATING: 7
This is my favorite abstract game. There's room for short-term impluse and long-term strategy. This is a good game to play with families; it's interactive without feeling too cutthroat.

RATING: 8
Ingenious is a very elegant game. Its easy to learn, quick to play and oddly satisfying. There is nothing quite so fun as calling ingenious three times in one round. This has become an instant favourite among our friends and family.

The one thing that brings the score down is the luck factor. It can be frustrating to watch other people rack up points while you have no decent tiles in your hand. You can mitigate this by dumping your whole tile set at the end of a turn, but you could end up with an equally bad set of new tiles.

RATING: 5
This game occupies an uncomfortable middle ground for me: The game is a bit too light and strategically shallow considering its length and fiddliness.

Actually, I prefer dominoes over this (in particular, the four player block game in which all 28 dominoes are dealt out). At least a round of dominoes ends in at most seven turns. 

As for other multiplayer abstracts I'd rather play: Octiles, Rumis/Blokus 3D, and Kaliko. These three are similar to Ingenious whereby players add pieces to a common structure of pieces. However, in these three games, the structure being built is far more intriguing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
This is a simple abstract based on dominoes. Players take turns placing double hexagons with two symbols on them and scoring points for matching the symbols to hexagons already played. The first level of play is to place your piece (you have a choice of six) to score the most points. The second, and I was only just begining to see how you might do this at the end of my first game wis to place to prevent your opponent scoring well from your play. Is there more to it than that? I'm not sure. I don't think this game has enough depth to be a really interesting abstract and I don't think it is 'fun' enough to be a family game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Probably the best game I know in what I call the "family abstract" genre (an abstract game  usually with some small element of chance, which can handle more than 2 players, has simple rules and colourful pieces and seems to be aimed at the family market). The scoring method - that your final score is the lowest of your 6 individual-colour scores - is really the only interesting thing going on here, but it absolutely makes the game. This is even a pretty good thoughtful game for 2 - there is of course luck in the tile draw, but I'm sure better players will win more often. With more, it tends to be more random. But I haven't yet got to the main problem with this game - that comes when one plays it with non-gamers, which unfortunately will probably be the case most of the time this hits the table. Then most people aren't interested in blocking, but just try to score the highest they can. If everyone else is doing this, you basically have to go along with them, and then it comes down to pure luck...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Seems like a decent abstract game, wasn't a bad game, but it wasn't "fun" for me.

RATING: 6
----------

I like to play ingenious... which actually means a LOT coming from someone who doesn't care too much for abstracts.  This is my favorite abstract (by far).  It's a great light game that can be picked up and played pretty quickly, and it can be pretty addicting.

I probably prefer it with 2 players because you have much more control over your own fate and you must truly focus on blocking more than grabbing points to win.  Plus, inexperienced players will accidentally help the following person by leaving huge scoring runs open.

My parents, who are non gamers, love Ingenious.  They've NEVER been into any kind of board games before... especially my dad.  But after playing Ingenious, he asked me to bring it back by the house next time I come visit.  That's huge.  My wife, however, thinks the game is incredibly boring.  She has no desire to play any kind of abstract.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Moderately Accessible Light Euro 

One of my favorites! 7% of my collection is Designed by Dr. Knizia, by far the most represented Designer. Here is his famous scoring mechanism (highest lowest score) in its purest form.

I love how the game seems like it should be quite confrontational, but the conflict is dissipated almost to nothing though the abstract nature of the pieces and the wide open beginning--indeed, for many games no pressure at all will be felt until the last 10 or so turns, it feels like all at once someone applies a clamp to the possibilities! For the beginning, it almost feels as if the game is co-operative, everyone building off of each other, which is wonderful. With more play, strategy can play a larger part, and the game gets understandable from further out as one monitors all of the other players color levels for weaknesses.

I make people say 'INGENIOUS' when they reach 18, of course. How could I resist such drama? My kids are terrible at the game, but still they love it, singing Ingenious out with epic delight.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
Nice abstract. Allows for some strategic planning without hurting your head. Players get a rack of six tiles and a score chart. The score chart tracks your scores for each of the six colors. Scoring is of the "your overall score is the score of your weakest color" variety, so it's important to keep balance your acquisitions. However, there is a bonus turn given to each player whenever you get a color score up to 18, so if it gives you another turn and gets rid of a lousy tile, play it! On your turn you play and draw back up to six. Each tile has two colors on it, in a double-hex arrangement. To play you place one of your tiles on the board, where one of the colors is adjacent to another hex with that color. You score points for each color match, and more if you can add on to existing lines of color. Player scores are open, so you should look for opportunities to shut out color runs that will help your opponents catch up to you. Very simple to play.

I've played a kajillion online games versus AI, but not too many F2F plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

It's interesting, but doesn't seem as deep as Tzaar for instance. Not that simple or immediate as Indigo. And not that tense as many 2 players abstracts can be. Sorry, but this didn't deserve a SdJ win. It's interesting, it's good, but kinda flat.

RATING: 9
A simple abstract where you lay tiles to match up coloured symbols. You have a different scoring track for each unique symbol; the symbol you scored the worst for is your final score. Do you cap off a run of symbols to prevent others from cashing in, or do you keep a chain going in hopes that you can score multiple times? Long term planning is limited, though there is plenty of opportunity to make decisions. Some randomness in the tile draw, though superior play should overcome any luck inherent in the game. I always want to play this one "one more time."

RATING: 7.5
A clever abstact with the usual Reiner twist. Players play tiles with 2 of 6 symbols from their pool of 6. Each turn, they place a tile and score 1 point for linking like colours in a row or adjacent to the new tile. Once the board is full, the game ends and players look at their weakest scoring colour...and in T&E style, the one with the highest scoring lowest colour wins!

The game is a fairly fast playing abstract that offers a good amount of tension, nasty play and thought. Very elegant. I like it...which is saying a lot considering that abstracts and kniza is a combination I'd usually avoid.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First impression: Not only is Ingenious the best multiplayer abstract I've ever played, but it's a fun game that can hold its own against non-abstracts. It's also loads of fun with two, but it suffers from chance elements so much that games are often decided by it. Chance in an abstract is a double-edged sword, as on the one hand it makes the game more engaging to a broader audience, introduces a great amount of tension, and ensures variety; but on the other hand you can get screwed by the draw. This especially hurts in a two player game. In such a game, each player can play more or less optimally, and the winner can be mostly decided by tile draws in the late game. This makes the game unsatisfactory even for the winner, as it's no fun to win when this is obviously the result of luck - not in an abstract, at any rate. The best way to mitigate the chance element is to play 4 players with teams. In this case, each team has a total of 12 tiles. But the most fun to be had with this game is with the regular 3 and 4 person games. Luck still plays a role, but sort of screws everyone equally - or at least it feels that way!

Ingenious shares some qualities with Knizia's Tigris and Euphrates, notably the victory condition (you win by ensuring that your lowest scoring color/shape is higher than everyone else's lowest color/shape), but also somewhat in the tile drawing and laying departments. Your choices in Ingenious are much more constrained than they are in T&E, however, and so Ingenious is not nearly as meaty a game. But because it's a fun, light abstract, most of the groups I play with are going to prefer Ingenious to T&E (even though I think the latter is a more satisfying game).

For a two player abstract, I prefer YINSH or Go, both of which I enjoy a lot. But if you like a bit of luck, you'll probably prefer Ingenious even for two players.

Update (after many, many plays): Okay, my initial take on this game was a bit off. I think the two player game is the best way to play Ingenious. With three and four players, luck of the draw plays a more vital role, making the game a bit more chaotic. With more players, skillful play is not rewarded as consistently as it is in a two player game. I like Ingenious as a light, fun end of the day kind of game. You can play it purely on instinct, which is great for when your brain is fried or you are tired - and it serves well as a way to wind down the day with my wife. It's also a great abstract to play with kids - in a multiplayer situation, they actually have a chance to win.

RATING: N/A

Scoring system has written Knizia all over it which is does not have to be a bad thing. It works! 

Played the game with 3 players for the first time and had to rank it down one point. This game should have been made for 2 players only IMO. I don't want to play it differently anymore because the way things are supposed to work are simply better with 2 players. In my only single play with 3 players one of the players had to chose one player to give the victory to. King making by definition. Not for me.

Ingenious is excellent, for 2, 3 or 4 players. With couple friends, gaming buddies and family this has been met with a positive reception. In fact this comment reminds me that I should bring it to my parents more often, they enjoyed it and they complain that I try to teach them too many new games, so they don't get a chance to really learn them.

RATING: 8.5
Great abstract.  I cant tell how much of this is luck based yet, but it seems like you can play "better" than your opponent by making good choices with what tiles you have.  And you can strategically block your opponent to keep their score low.

This has been a big hit 2 player, but havent gotten a multi-player game together yet.

Update after 50+ 2-player plays against the iPad AI:  This game is insanely addictive, and the ipad beats me about 40-50% of the time.  I love it.

Update:  After over 100 plays on the iPad, some subtle strategic maneurvering starts to pay huge dividends.  You start to see how to score 5 points on a play instead of 4, which adds up later when the games are always close.  You start to watch the other guy's score more closely to seal off certain colors from being scored further.  You start to really watch both scores to plan your next move.  You learn to score a certain color at the same time as your opponent to stay ahead of him on each color.  All great stuff.

RATING: 7
Wow, a Knizia game I really like.  This abstract strategy game has the same overall attraction that Blokus has (IMO).  The rules are VERY easy to learn and the game plays pretty quick, but there is definitely a bit of strategy here.  The only gripe I have is you can have bad luck and just not get many tiles of a particular color (I only got 3 yellow tiles one game), meaning your score is going to be very low b/c your score is determined by whichever color you score lowest on.  One possible remedy might be to score your second to worst color, but I have not tried that yet.  Despite this gripe, I like the game a lot and think it's an excellent game, particularly if you like Blokus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
[FFG 'Ingenious' 2004 edition] {Abstract, couple; pattern-building, partnership} Ingenious is a nice abstract game for families because it's easy to learn and play. Besides, it has nice user interaction. And it's tight and exciting most of the time due to the hidden tile information.

Ingenious is a good gateway game in abstract domain. It's not a perfect-information game; thus, the size of decision tree is limited and players are forced to take calculated risks. As a result, Ingenious is a fast-paced game without the deep analysis-thinking.  In addition, scoring happens each turn and it's easier for player to visualize and understand the board, unlike other deep abstract games in which the game situation is usually opaque until late game.

As said, Ingenious is not a genuine abstract game, which should have perfect information. Player needs a little luck in drawing tiles.  Its uniqueness is built around the special scoring rule (though it is clearly derived from E&T). Due to its scoring rule, Ingenious is tight enough that all players have certain chance to win, even though they are lagging behind. This dynamic nature of Ingenious may upset the pure-abstract game lovers, though.

Players have several directions of decision each time, but not agonizing: rising inferior color, shooting for 'ingenious', developing a new base, trying to flush your hand, blocking others, or developing other spaces? Reserving high-score color at late game to score for 'Ingenious' to take multiple moves at late game?

Game parts are of good quality and durable except the game trays and game board should be shaped to hold the game pieces more firmly.

RATING: 7.5
Knizia's Ingenious has interesting scoring mechanic: you must increase the score of your lowest scoring color/shape on the board and match it with the scores to move ahead in overall victory points. This creates a current that pushes game play and adds a bit of tension to an abstract game.
