RATING: 7
I've found it to really be a good two, three, and four player game - best with four players in teams of two. Interesting abstract game with classic Knizia scoring system - your best low score determines winner. But wait, if you max out a color, you get a bonus turn, so there is an incentive to go nuts on at least one color. But again, if you do that, you may leave on color too far back. I've dropped the rating slightly after playing the computer version a lot. There is a lot of luck despite having a selection of tiles. Get the right string of tiles, and you can shut people out while running the board and there is nothing that can be done. Not as bad when playing teams because a bad run for you isn't as likely to happen at the same time to your partner.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
Fantastic strategy game. The rules are quickly understood but I must admit that all the strategic elements took a while to reveal themselves to me - the game has got better as I have understood it more. 

There are many aspects to it, you might choose to attempt to sabotage one or more opponents or you can concentrate on your own game. It's very dynamic as you can plan a couple of moves ahead but the actions of an opponent can change your plans quite quickly. 

Best played, in my opinion, with three players.

RATING: 8
This baby's a harsh mistress. It can give a big hug one turn, and punch in the face the next. It can be as simple or as complex as you like it to be. It can be an all-out scorefest or a drawn-out war of attrition.

I love the fact that after dozens of 2-player games, I can still discover new ways of playing, and layers of complexity become apparent.

However, luck of the draw becomes more prominent at endgame, especially in 3- or 4-player games. So much so that between players of a similar level, the winner will be more than likely determined by lucky or unlucky draws. That's a bit disheartening, especially when you happen to win by not playing optimally and you lose when trying hard to work something out.

I'll play a game anytime, with any number of players.

27-AUG-2012: Upgraded to 7.5 and mainly 2 player games.

27-DEC-2012: Upgraded to 8.

RATING: 6.75
There are several reasons to like this game - the winning condition (score your lowest color) makes for challenging decisions; the solitaire variant and puzzles add flexibility (and make it a great game to bring early to a public game night); and it's a non-threatening abstract for new gamers.  The attractive color and design of the pieces are a draw, and the fact that there is no elimination or isolation lets everyone stay in the game and be reasonably competitive from their first play.

RATING: 7
Nifty little abstract that plays quickly and lightly.  Nice skillful game. I'd like to try out the partnership variant, because all partnership variants are cool.  Scoring is a bit wacky to get used to, but  still very manageable.  It plays much lighter than it is(which is a good thing).  After 10 plays its getting a bump.  Its not the greatest game ever, but with two players it has fun decisions.  Limit your opponents ingeniouses, block off the opponents lowest, set up your own ingeniouses.  Its probably loses a point with each player added, but its quite the fun 2 player game.  For some reason, I just can't figure out the proper balance between offense and defense.  I'm always too skewed in one direction.  Just another reason why the game is fun.

RATING: 8
Played at PrezCon 05.  I enjoyed this one, so I probably need to give up on my preconceptions about abstract games.  Sooooo many abstract games are perfect information 2 player games that I really needed to try something outside that mold.  This game plays fairly quickly, and as usual, I enjoy the Knizia balanced scoring mechanism.  I got a 2 and a 4 player game in, and I think the variable board size for the number of players is very nice, too.  It seems nicely balanced for different numbers of players.  I think I may be biased, though.  I generally enjoy theme in a game, and my interest in Genial is muted by the abstract nature.  It was good fun, and given time, my interest level could sneak up on me.

As I suspected, this game has continued to grow on me.  Any disinterest I had due to it's abstract nature is gone now.

RATING: 8
Abstract tile-placement strategy game easily scalable for 2 to 4 players that is also playable in partnership or as a solitaire puzzle. The goal is to score points by positioning tile colors/symbols adjacent to similar ones already on the board. Balance in scoring is primordial as your final score is equal to your worst scoring color/symbol. Easy to learn but extremely solid. Tile draws maintain a level of randomness that forces players to adapt and increases replayability. Should appeal to many categories of gamers - use as a family or gateway game or with seasoned players. Component quality is top-notch.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
There is something almost relaxing about Ingenious. Unlike many other abstracts (for example Yinsh, which melted part of my brain last week) there is little pressure to try to think ahead or visualize board development. I am sure that one could play Ingenious that way, but the game itself lulls the players into an easy gait, a lazy "summer on the front porch swing" kind of playing. I've now seen the game approached with both relentless, bitter blocking, and alternatively an almost gentle cooperation-- and even at both of these extremes the game still feels soft, casual. I cannot pinpoint what part of the system softens the edges; I don't think it is the luck of the tile draw. It is more as if, since there are so many ways to score so many things, there is a feel good vibe however one plays. No matter what you do (practically) there is the warm-fuzzy of moving cubes, of building something. While this is probably reading too much into things, there is also something warm and fuzzy about winning with your loser: it is as if the narrative drive of the game gets everyone focused on the hollywood-esque goal of making the underdog do-good. I think it is this gentleness, the warmth of the game if you will, that makes it such a perfect game to bring out with non-gamers. I had an old curmudgeon math teacher who liked to complain that "most people would rather die than think. And most do." Ingenious is a great game for most: you can play it without thinking too much and not have an embarrassing result. This is amplified by the winning condition focusing on the losing piece. In many ways I'd like to give this a 9 or a 10: you can teach it in under 5 minutes, a very broad spectrum of people will enjoy it, and there are interesting decisions along the way. But what keeps this from that lofty rating is that it isn't something I'd pull out with gamers. There just isn't enough tension. Its softness is, well, too soft. But if I can only pack one or two games for a mixed crowd, this probably would be in the bag.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Oct 2017: Rating +2
Why is my rating going up?  This game stands the test of time.  It has no theme that's going to be "last year's news" and no artwork that's going to become obsolete.  It's simple.  It's easy to teach to anyone.  It can be competitive enough to challenge seasoned gamers.  It works well at all player counts.  Yes, there can often be one player who excels and puts everyone else to shame, so you need to consider the dynamics of the group.  Don't play it with a sore loser who wants to quit the second someone pulls ahead, but do play it with people who see competition as a reason to up effort!   Ingenious is definitely one of my favorite games within the abstract genre.

Original, Dec 2016:
I give it a 6 only because I don't enjoy it as well as numerous other games.  It's a blend of strategy and luck of the draw, which a decent balance of both.  However, it is a game that gets boring to me if played too often.  It feels like a light and easy game until I play it with someone who heavily dominates, making me just wish it would end faster.  I don't mind losing, but I hate feeling like there's no possible strategy that could help me recover.

Would probably be a nice gateway game for kids or adults alike, as the rules are very quick and easy to teach.  My parents love this one and have also added it to their collection.

RATING: 5
Immensely good as a gateway game. Best with 2 (or with two teams of 2). The strategy consists in getting ahead in a color and then blocking it from being available on the board. Luck plays a role of course but considering the time commitment it isn't really an issue. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Knizia; Category: Abstract Strategy; Mechanics: Tile Placement, Pattern Building,Hand Management; When a player placed a piece, they are trying to line up multiples of the same symbol. When you do so, you score points for that symbol, which is tracked on your player mat. If you max out your score in a particular color, you get an extra turn. Now here's the twist: at the end of the game, everyone compares their LOWEST score and that's their ending score. So if you end the game with 18 in everything except red which has 12, your score is twelve. (If you've played Tigris and Euphrates, you've seen this before.) Other names: Mensa Connections, Genius, Genial, Einfach Genial, Genial Connections, Helt genialt!, Ganske enkelt genialt, Preprosto Genialno, Genialt, Geniale
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Anyone who knows a little about Reiner Knizia’s games will know that the good Doctor loves games that deal with trying to get points in various different categories and then only score that category in which the player has the fewest. In the past, Knizia has used this mechanic to develop highly complicated games, but with Einfach Genial, he has distilled the mechanic down to its purest form.
\13\\13\
The game is played on a hex board. 120 equally sized pieces, each consisting of two joined hexes, come with the game. There are symbols on each hex that make up the piece - some pieces have two identical symbols, some have two different symbols (not unlike dominoes). The goal of the game is, through clever placement, to obtain points in the different symbol colors. Points are claimed by placing a piece such that the symbols on it lie next to already-placed pieces with the same symbol.
\13\\13\
The game ends when no more tiles can be placed onto the board or when a player reaches the maximum number in every color. Now each player looks to see how many points they scored in the colour they 'scored the least'. Whoever has the most points in their least-scored colour is the winner. Simple.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Fun,  easy to pick up game.   Simple rules.  The "score your lowest color" is always a tricky thing.   Scoring the tiles takes a little explaining,  but otherwise the rules are dead easy,  but everyone usually agrees that there's lots of strategy.The board is ...uh...plain (which is really just a kind way of saying that it is very,  very ugly).  The pieces are plastic,  and although a bit lightweight,  they're quite nice.  Would have been nice to have a plastic board with grooves like Blokus.  Score sheets are also on nice,  thick stock but share the aesthetics of the board.  I think it's the off-white color, that makes them look like poor paper photocopies.  Scoring cubes are a bit small - be careful, a good jostle would send everything flying.

Inventoried the tiles - there 5 of each of the double-coloured ones (5x6),  and 6 of all the other types (6x15).

RATING: 10
Played numerous times face-to-face, and hundreds of times on BSW (mainly with 2). I liked my first play of that game, but did not suspect I would like it that much after so many plays. The rules are really simple, the game is rather nice-looking (I am aware that could be discussed), the luck factor is reduced a lot by the possibility to change your tiles (which is much more useful than some people seem to imagine), and the game plays rather quickly, turning it into a very nice filler. Also, the board scales very well (although I much prefer playing with 2, or with teams, to prevent "blocking responsibility" problems). Overall, a simple, yet impressively enjoyable design.

RATING: 7.5
A really good abstract. I have played this many times. I can play this with almost anybody and they enjoy it. Can be played on several different levels. I like to play it with a focus on accumulating points with less experienced gamers and I focus more on the blocking aspects when playing with more experienced or competitive gamers. I think this will become a classic abstract.

This is not a perfect information game. There is a large amount of luck in the draw of tiles. This is unpleasent to some people but makes the game easy to introduce to novice game players.

RATING: 8
This is a very fun, nice looking abstract game where you get points by connecting lines of tiles.  An easy to set up, fast playing game with simple rules and a fair amount of strategy. I like the scoring twist: player with the most points in their least scored color wins so you can't totally ignore any colors (but comparing your scores to your opponents will let you know when you can start ignoring something!).  And it scales well from Solitaire to 4 players (unlike Blokus which only seems to work well for 4 and okay for 2)  Definitely will continue to get plenty of play at our house!
